We need to characterize the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors. Let's denote S = {N : N has at least three proper divisors}. So it seems that numbers that are multiples of 6 and divisible by 4 may map to numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5? For 72, it maps to 78 which is not divisible by 4 or 5. So maybe the condition for a1 to be possible is that a1 is either a fixed point (multiple of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) or a number that maps to a fixed point in one step. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point F. That is, f(N)=F, where F is a multiple of 6 with no factor 4 or 5. Then the sequence becomes constant from a2 onward. So we need to solve for N: N/2 + d2 + d3 = F.

We can try to characterize such N. Since d2 and d3 are proper divisors of N, they must be at most N/2. So F >= N/2, so N <= 2F. Also, d2 and d3 are at least 1 and at least something. Typically, if N is even, then N/2 is a divisor. The next largest proper divisor d2: if N is divisible by 3, then N/3 is a divisor; if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor; etc. So we need to consider the possible values of d2 and d3 based on N's prime factors.

Maybe we can prove that the only N that map to a fixed point are those where N is a multiple of 6 and N/2 is the sum of two divisors that are exactly N/3 and N/6? Actually for N=72, we have N/2=36, d2=26? Wait, 72's divisors:1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,36. Three largest:36,24,18 sum=78. So d2=24, d3=18. Then d2+d3=42 = N/2? Actually N/2=36, not 42. So for 72, f=78, not N. So the condition for N to map to F is that N/2 + d2 + d3 = F. For N=72, N/2=36, d2=24, d3=18, sum=78. So that works.

We can try to solve generally. Let N be composite with at least three proper divisors. Then f(N) = N/2 + d2 + d3. We want f(N) = F, where F is a fixed point: F = 6k, k odd, 5∤k. Also, note that F must have at least three proper divisors, which it does.

We can try to find all N such that f(N) = F. Since N and F are related, we might be able to show that N must be a multiple of 6 and N/2 must be a divisor of something. Actually from the definition of f, we have that N/2, d2, d3 are all divisors of N. So N is divisible by 2, and also by d2 and d3. So N is divisible by lcm(2,d2,d3). Since d2 and d3 are proper divisors, they are less than N.

Maybe we can approach by considering the condition that the sequence is infinite. That means that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit under f never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since f(N) can be larger than N, the orbit could potentially increase and eventually become prime or square of prime. But we can try to prove that if N is not a fixed point, then the orbit must eventually hit a prime or a prime square. The only way to avoid that is if the orbit reaches a fixed point. So the set of possible a1 is the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point under f. That is, the set of all numbers whose f-orbit is eventually constant at some fixed point.

Thus, we need to characterize the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. This might be an infinite set, but perhaps we can describe it as all numbers that are multiples of 6? So maybe it's all numbers that are multiples of 6 and have at most two distinct prime factors? Or something like that.

Let's examine the fixed points: they are numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5. So they are numbers where the prime factors are 2 and 3, and possibly other primes but with exponent 1? Actually 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5 means N=2*3*k where k is odd and not divisible by 5. So the prime factors of k can be any odd primes except 5. So fixed points can have many prime factors, as long as they are not divisible by 5. But they must not be divisible by 4, so the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. And they must not be divisible by 5, so the exponent of 5 is 0. So the set of fixed points is all numbers that are twice an odd number that is not divisible by 5, and also divisible by 3? Actually N=6k, so N is divisible by 3. So fixed points are numbers divisible by 6, with exactly one factor of 2, and no factor of 5. So fixed points are numbers where the exponent of 2 is exactly 1, the exponent of 5 is 0, and the exponent of 3 is at least 1? Actually N must be divisible by 6, so it must have at least one factor of 2 and one factor of 3. So yes, exponent of 2 >=1, exponent of 3 >=1. And since N cannot be divisible by 4, exponent of 2 is exactly 1. So fixed points are exactly numbers of the form 2 * 3^a * m, where a >= 1, m is odd and not divisible by 5, and also m is coprime to 2 and 3? Actually m can contain 3? Actually if u contains 3, then we could increase a. So we can assume gcd(u,6)=1. So fixed points are numbers N such that v2(N)=1, v5(N)=0, and v3(N) >= 1.

Thus, the condition for a1 to be valid is that a1 is a multiple of 6, and that when we write a1 = 2^e * 3^a * m with m not divisible by 2 or 3, we have:
- e is odd (e >= 1) and v5(a1)=0 (so m not divisible by 5).
- a >= (e+1)/2? Actually we need that after (e-1)/2 steps of applying f(N)=13N/12, we get a number with v2=1 and v3 >= 1. That requires that a - (e-1)/2 >= 1 => a >= (e+1)/2. So a >= (e+1)/2.
Also, if e=1, then we have a fixed point only if v5=0, so a >= 1, which is automatically true for multiples of 6. So the condition includes fixed points.

But is it also necessary that the number never becomes odd? If e is odd and >=3, then we apply f(N)=13N/12 each step, which reduces e by 2 and a by 1. Since e is odd, after (e-1)/2 steps, we get e=1. During these steps, the exponent of 3 decreases by 1 each time, and we need that it never becomes 0 before we reach e=1. That is exactly a >= (e+1)/2. So that ensures that at the step when e=1, a is still at least 1. So the final number is a multiple of 6 with e=1, a>=1, and v5=0, so it's a fixed point. So the sequence becomes constant at that fixed point. So such a1 are valid.

What about numbers with e=1 but v5>=1? They become odd and likely fail. So excluded.

What about numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 3? That's impossible because multiples of 6 are divisible by 3. So all multiples of 6 have v3>=1.

Thus, the set of all possible a1 is precisely the set of positive integers N such that:
- N is divisible by 6.
- The exponent of 2 in N is odd.
- The exponent of 5 in N is 0.
- The exponent of 3 in N is at least (exponent of 2 + 1)/2.

We can try to solve generally. Let N be composite with at least three proper divisors. Then f(N) = N/2 + d2 + d3. We want f(N) = F, where F is a fixed point: F = 6k, k odd, 5∤k. Also, note that F must have at least three proper divisors, which it does.

We can try to find all N such that f(N) = F. Since N and F are related, we might be able to show that N must be a multiple of 6 and N/2 must be a divisor of something. Maybe we can approach by considering the condition that the sequence is infinite. That means that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. Let's solve f(N)=102. We need N/2 + d2 + d3 = 102. Since N is even, let N=2m. Then f(N)=m + d2 + d3 = 102. d2 and d3 are proper divisors of 2m. The largest proper divisor is m. Let's examine the mapping. For N even, f(N) = N/2 + d2 + d3. If N is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, then f(N)=N. If N is a multiple of 6 but divisible by 4, then f(N) > N. And sometimes f(N) is a fixed point. For N=504, f(N)=546, which is a fixed point. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That is, N/2 + d2 + d3 = F, where F is a number with v2=1, v3>=1, v5=0.

We can try to solve this equation by considering the structure of N. Since d2 and d3 are proper divisors of N, they are of the form N/p for some divisors p. Usually, the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, and N/6 (if N has no other divisors in that range). So for numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, we have f(N)=N. For numbers that are multiples of 6 but divisible by 4 or 5, the three largest proper divisors might be N/2, N/4, N/3? Actually we need to sort properly. For N even, the largest proper divisor is N/2. The second largest is the largest divisor less than N/2. If N is divisible by 3, then N/3 is a divisor. If N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor. Since N/3 > N/4 if N>0, so the second largest is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N, etc. So for numbers that are multiples of 6, we have N/2 and N/3 as divisors. The third largest proper divisor could be N/4 if 4|N, else N/5 if 5|N, else N/6, etc. But note that N/6 is a divisor if 6|N. So for N divisible by 6, the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, and then the next largest is max of N/4 (if 4|N), N/5 (if 5|N), N/6 (if 6|N), etc. Since N/6 is always a divisor if 6|N, but we need to compare N/6 with N/4 and N/5. N/6 < N/4 because 1/6 < 1/4, and N/6 < N/5 because 1/6 < 1/5. So N/6 is smaller than N/4 and N/5. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor, and it is greater than N/6, so the third largest is N/4, not N/6. If N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then N/5 is a divisor, and N/5 > N/6? Since 1/5 > 1/6, yes. So third largest is N/5. If N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then the third largest is N/6. So we can characterize:

For N even and divisible by 3:
- If v2(N) >= 2 (i.e., N divisible by 4), then the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/4.
- Else if v5(N) >= 1 (i.e., N divisible by 5), then the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/5.
- Else, the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/6.

Thus, f(N) = 
- If v2(N) >= 2: N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/4) = N*13/12 = 13N/12.
- If v2(N) = 1 and v5(N) >= 1: N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/5) = N*(15/30+10/30+6/30) = N*31/30 = 31N/30.
- If v2(N) = 1 and v5(N) = 0: N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/6) = N*1 = N.

So, for N even and divisible by 3, f(N) is determined by the prime factorization. Note that we also require that N has at least three proper divisors, which is true if N is composite and not a prime square. For N even and divisible by 3, N is composite. If N is a prime square? For N=9, it's odd, not even. For N=25, not even. So no issue.

Thus, we have a complete description of f(N) for even N that are multiples of 3. But what about N that are even and divisible by 3 but not divisible by 4 or 5? That's the fixed point case. So indeed, the fixed points are exactly those N with v2(N)=1 and v5(N)=0 and v3(N)>=1? Actually they need to be divisible by 3, so yes, v3(N)>=1. And also need that the three largest are N/2,N/3,N/6, which requires that no divisor larger than N/6 exists besides N/2,N/3. That condition is v2(N)=1 (so N not divisible by 4) and v5(N)=0 (so N not divisible by 5). Also, note that if N has a divisor like N/7? That is less than N/6? Since 1/7 < 1/6, so N/7 is smaller than N/6. So it's fine. So the condition is exactly v2=1 and v5=0 and v3>=1. So fixed points are numbers with v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1. That includes 6=2*3, 18=2*3^2, 42=2*3*7, 54=2*3^3, 66=2*3*11, 78=2*3*13, 102=2*3*17, 114=2*3*19, 126=2*3^2*7, 138=2*3*23, etc. So that's the set.

Now, for N that are even and divisible by 3 but v2(N)>=2, we have f(N)=13N/12. That is > N. So the sequence increases. For N that are even and divisible by 3 but v2(N)=1 and v5(N)>=1, we have f(N)=31N/30, which is also > N. So the sequence increases. So if a1 is not a fixed point, then a2 = f(a1) > a1. And a2 is either of the form 13N/12 or 31N/30. Since N is even and divisible by 3, we can compute a2. Then we need that a2 also has at least three proper divisors, and the sequence continues. So we need to find all a1 such that the sequence eventually reaches a fixed point. Since the sequence is increasing, it could potentially diverge to infinity? But from examples, it eventually becomes constant at a fixed point. So we need to find all a1 such that after some steps, the sequence lands on a fixed point. That is, the orbit under f eventually becomes constant. Since f is multiplicative in a sense, we can analyze the iterates.

Let’s define the transformation for N even and divisible by 3. Write N = 2^e * 3^a * m, where m is not divisible by 2 or 3, and also we consider the condition for f. For N to have at least three proper divisors, we need N > 6? Actually N=6 works as fixed point. So we can assume N>=6.

We have three cases:
Case 1: e=1 and v5(N)=0. Then f(N)=N, fixed point.
Case 2: e=1 and v5(N)>=1. Then f(N)=31N/30. Note that this is > N, and the exponent of 2 remains 1? Because 31N/30 = (31/30)*N, and since 30=2*3*5, we have N=2*3^a*m, with m not divisible by 2 or 3. Then f(N)=31 * 2 * 3^a * m / (2*3*5) = 31 * 3^{a-1} * m / 5. So the exponent of 2 becomes 0 if a>=1? Wait, after division, we have no factor of 2? Because 2 cancels. So f(N) is odd? Actually N has exactly one factor of 2, so N=2*K. Then f(N)=31N/30 = (31/30)*2K = (31*2K)/(30) = (31K)/(15). Since 30=2*3*5, we get f(N)=31K/(15). But K = 3^a * m. So f(N) = 31 * 3^a * m / (15) = 31 * 3^{a-1} * m / 5. So the exponent of 2 in f(N) is 0, meaning f(N) is odd. So after one step, we get an odd number. Then the sequence may continue, but odd numbers are not divisible by 2, so their largest proper divisor is not N/2. So we need to analyze f for odd numbers.

Thus, numbers that are even and divisible by 3 but with v2=1 and v5>=1 lead to an odd number after one step. That odd number may have at least three proper divisors? Possibly it could be a prime or a prime square. For example, a1=30: N=30=2*3*5, e=1, v5=1, so f=31*30/30=31. 31 is prime, fails. a1=60: N=60=2^2*3*5, e=2, v5=1, so f=13*60/12=65. 65=5*13, v2=0, v5=1.
